        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.7b08870"><meta property="og:title" content="In Situ Atomic Force Microscopic Studies of Single Tin Nanoparticle: Sodiation and Desodiation in Liquid Electrolyte"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/acsami.7b08870.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Probing electrodes at a nanometer scale is challenging but desirable to reveal the structural evolution of materials in electrochemical reactions. Herein, we present an atomic force microscopic method for an in situ analysis of a single Sn nanoparticle during sodiation and desodiation, which is conducted in an aprotic liquid electrolyte akin to a real electrochemical environment of the Na-ion cells. The morphological evolution of different-sized single Sn nanoparticle is visualized during the charge/discharge cycles by using a homemade planar electrode. All of the Sn nanoparticles exhibit a dramatic initial volume expansion of about 420% after sodiation to Na15Sn4. Interestingly, we find that the smaller Sn nanoparticles show a lower rate of irreversible volume change and a better shape maintenance than the larger ones after desodiation. This finding suggests the importance of downsizing in improving the mechanical stability and the cycling performance of the Sn-based anodes in sodium-ion batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/acsami.7b08870.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da8dfe2da1b325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8dfe2da1b325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '1fd0b5be-6afb-490b-85fb-9a6d0ed1230d'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90da8dfe2da1b325-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8dfe2da1b325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

title="Download Hi-Res Image" target="_blank" class="figure-viewer_hi-res-link"><i class="icon-file-image-o"></i><span>Download Hi-Res Image</span></a><span class="fv-separator"></span><a href="#" title="Download to MS-PowerPoint" class="figure-viewer_ms-p-link"><i class="icon-file-powerpoint-o"></i><span>Download to MS-PowerPoint</span></a><span class="cit-this-container"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b08870&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acsami.7b08870" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite <script nonce="90da8dfe2da1b325-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ Atomic Force Microscopic Studies of Single Tin Nanoparticle: Sodiation and Desodiation in Liquid Electrolyte", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b08870", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ Atomic Force Microscopic Studies of Single Tin Nanoparticle: Sodiation and Desodiation in Liquid Electrolyte", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b08870", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ Atomic Force Microscopic Studies of Single Tin Nanoparticle: Sodiation and Desodiation in Liquid Electrolyte", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b08870", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ Atomic Force Microscopic Studies of Single Tin Nanoparticle: Sodiation and Desodiation in Liquid Electrolyte", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b08870", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ Atomic Force Microscopic Studies of Single Tin Nanoparticle: Sodiation and Desodiation in Liquid Electrolyte", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b08870", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ Atomic Force Microscopic Studies of Single Tin Nanoparticle: Sodiation and Desodiation in Liquid Electrolyte", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b08870", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ Atomic Force Microscopic Studies of Single Tin Nanoparticle: Sodiation and Desodiation in Liquid Electrolyte", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b08870", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ Atomic Force Microscopic Studies of Single Tin Nanoparticle: Sodiation and Desodiation in Liquid Electrolyte", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b08870", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ Atomic Force Microscopic Studies of Single Tin Nanoparticle: Sodiation and Desodiation in Liquid Electrolyte", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b08870", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/medium/am-2017-08870h_0005.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/large/am-2017-08870h_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b08870&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Probing electrodes at a nanometer scale is challenging but desirable to reveal the structural evolution of materials in electrochemical reactions. Herein, we present an atomic force microscopic method for an in situ analysis of a single Sn nanoparticle during sodiation and desodiation, which is conducted in an aprotic liquid electrolyte akin to a real electrochemical environment of the Na-ion cells. The morphological evolution of different-sized single Sn nanoparticle is visualized during the charge/discharge cycles by using a homemade planar electrode. All of the Sn nanoparticles exhibit a dramatic initial volume expansion of about 420% after sodiation to Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>. Interestingly, we find that the smaller Sn nanoparticles show a lower rate of irreversible volume change and a better shape maintenance than the larger ones after desodiation. This finding suggests the importance of downsizing in improving the mechanical stability and the cycling performance of the Sn-based anodes in sodium-ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D28620%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DMo%2BHan%252C%2BChenbo%2BZhu%252C%2BQing%2BZhao%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D9%26issueNum%3D34%26contentID%3Dacsami.7b08870%26title%3DIn%2BSitu%2BAtomic%2BForce%2BMicroscopic%2BStudies%2Bof%2BSingle%2BTin%2BNanoparticle%253A%2BSodiation%2Band%2BDesodiation%2Bin%2BLiquid%2BElectrolyte%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D28626%26publicationDate%3DAugust%2B2017" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright Â© 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290920&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanoparticles" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanoparticles" class="article__tags__link">Nanoparticles</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292500&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Particle size" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Particle%20size" class="article__tags__link">Particle class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i                         of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Single+Nanoparticle&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Irreversible+Volume+Change&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_irreversible%20volume%20change">irreversible volume change</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div class="extra-info-sec articleNote"><h4><i class="icon-note-flag" aria-hidden="true"></i>Note</h4><p class="first last">This paper contains enhanced objects.</p></div><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">As one of the most promising anode materials in the sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 1 to 8">(1-8)</a> Sn is cheap and safe with a high capacity (847 mAh g<sup>â1</sup> for Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 9 to 13">(9-13)</a> However, Sn suffers from a theoretical volume variation of 420% when fully sodiated in the alloying process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> The large volume expansion causes cracks and pulverization after cycling, resulting in an electrical disconnection between Sn and current collector, which, in turn, leads to a rapid fading of the capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 15 to 17">(15-17)</a> To accommodate the volume expansion and suppress the fracture of Sn anode, extensive researches have been focused on fabricating nanosized Sn-based materials, including Sn-based nanoalloys, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 18 to 20">(18-20)</a> three-dimensional (3D) array of Sn nanorods, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Sn/C nanocomposites, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22, 23)</a> and so on. It is therefore of fundamental importance to understand how the particle size influences the cycling performance. An in situ characterization technique is urgently required to investigate the size-dependent morphological evolution of Sn nanoparticles in the charge/discharge cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p">In the past few years, in situ techniques have brought significant progress in the electrochemical researches. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 24 to 32">(24-32)</a> Huang and co-workers reported the structural changes and the phase transformations of Sn nanoparticles (size between 100 and 300 nm) during fast electrochemical sodiation using in situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> More recently, Leeâs group explored the influence of electrically resistive Zintl ions in NaâSn batteries using an in situ-focused ion-beam system. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Previous studies indicate that the sodiation of Sn is complex, which could be influenced by the particle size, test environment, and even charging rates. The in situ studies mentioned above were performed in all of the solid-state systems. Because sodium-ion batteries usually work in a liquid electrolyte, characterization of the corresponding solidâliquid interface is required to study the morphological evolution and the interfacial properties of Sn sodiation/desodiation under the real working conditions of batteries. Atomic force microscopy (AFM) is a useful analytical technique to adapt liquid testing environment as in real cells. Moreover, AFM is nondestructive and can provide a wide range of spatial resolutions from nano- to micrometer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 34 to 37">(34-37)</a> When AFM is coupled with a potentiostat or galvanostat, the electrochemical reaction of the electrode materials occurs like in operating cells. Important surface changes of electrode, including morphological evolution, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 38 to 40">(38-40)</a> interfacial mechanisms, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 41 and 42">(41, 42)</a> and solidâelectrolyte interphase (SEI) formation, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 43 to 48">(43-48)</a> can be visualized during the in situ AFM scan in working conditions.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this article, we report an in situ AFM study of a single Sn nanoparticle sodiation and desodiation in a liquid electrolyte. By combining the voltammetry technique with the in situ AFM system on a rationally designed planar electrode, the volume change of Sn nanoparticles with different sizes is characterized at different potentials during the electrochemical reaction. As expected, all of the Sn nanoparticles (70â330 nm) investigated by the AFM reach a theoretical volume expansion of about 420% after sodiation. Interestingly, we find in the desodiation process that the irreversible volume change of nanoparticles is strongly size dependent, indicating the vital role of size controlling in the design of the Sn-based electrode materials.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> shows schematically the preparation of planar anode and in situ AFM setup for a single Sn nanoparticle characterization. In contrast to the complicated electrode fabrication techniques like electron beam lithography in previous studies, we use a simple spray painting method to prepare the planar electrode (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a). No binder or conductive carbon was used in the electrode. The Au film was first deposited as a current collector on a silica substrate by magnetron sputtering. The as-deposited film was flat, dense, and about 300 nm thick (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). Then, Sn nanoparticles with a high phase purity and a particle size of 50â400 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>) were firmly supported on the Au film using the spray painting <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> method. The particles were ultrasonically dispersed in ethanol, and the suspension of single particle was sprayed homogeneously on the Au film using an air brush. Finally, the planar anode was calcined in Ar to fix the Sn nanoparticles tightly on the current collector. The photograph of Sn electrode (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c) and the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of Sn nanoparticles assembled on the Au film (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figures 1">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S2c</a>) show that different-sized single Sn nanoparticle can be well dispersed and fixed on the planar Au film in a single-layer form. This simple method of fabricating planar electrode realizes the testing of a single particle on the electrode without the binder or carbon, which could also be applied to prepare other metal nanoparticle anodes with low melting point. The whole electrochemical testing system includes a single Sn particle planar electrode, a sodium electrode, and an AFM probe (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> shows the photograph of the in situ setup designed for the AFM measurement in liquid environment, where the sodium counter-electrode and the AFM probe are fixed and immersed in the electrolyte, whereas the Sn electrode moves through the transparent holder for data collection. By using an o-ring and two tubes for electrolyte filling, the liquid electrolyte (NaClO<sub>4</sub>-ethylene carbonate/propylene carbonate (EC/PC)) is sealed inside the holder and the in situ cell can keep working for several days.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/medium/am-2017-08870h_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/large/am-2017-08870h_0006.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Preparation schematics of a single Sn nanoparticle planar anode. (b) Schematic of the planar microscale battery analyzed by AFM in a liquid electrolyte for in situ measurements. (c) The photograph of Sn nanoparticle planar anode. (d) SEM image of single Sn nanoparticle on planar anode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/large/am-2017-08870h_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b08870&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a shows the morphological evolution of a single 180 nm Sn nanoparticle (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>) in the first cycle of sodiation and desodiation. To investigate the evolution of Sn nanoparticles at different potentials, linear sweep voltammetry is used in combination with potentiostatic method during the electrochemical testing. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> According to the cyclic voltammograms (CV) curve of the Sn nanoparticles (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b), potentiostatic holds at 0.50, 0.30, 0.15, and 0.01 V during the sodiation process and 0.24, 0.58, 0.71, 0.83, and 2.00 V during the desodiation process are selected for the measurement (a video of continuous sodiation/desodiation process can be found in <a href="/doi/media/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/am7b08870_weo_001.avi"></a><a href="/doi/media/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/am7b08870_weo_001.avi" class="ext-link">Supporting Movie 1</a>). The anode is held for 6 h under each potential to complete the Sn expansion and contraction. Representative electrochemical curves at different potentials from the potentiostatic method are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. In the sodiation process, swelling of Sn starts below 0.50 V and the maximum is observed between 0.15 and 0.01 V. After full sodiation, the nanoparticle is converted to Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> with a (220) interplanar distance of 0.46 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). Meanwhile, some byproducts show up on the Au film, which can be attributed to the decomposition of the electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> During the desodiation process, the particle contracts stably with the increase in potential. The morphology does not change drastically above 0.83 V and the particle cannot return to the original size after full charging.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/medium/am-2017-08870h_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/large/am-2017-08870h_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) In situ 3D AFM images of one Sn nanoparticle taken at selected electrochemical potentials during sodiation and desodiation in the first cycle. (b) Representative CV curve of Sn nanoparticles in the first cycle at a sweep rate of 0.1 mV/s. (c) Volume changes of the Sn nanoparticle taken at the selected electrochemical potentials.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/large/am-2017-08870h_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b08870&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c plots the volume change rate versus the applied potential in the first cycle. The volume change is calculated by using the analysis software based on the integral calculation and the details can be found in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>. Data of volume change are reported as the average values with error bars displayed in the figures. There is also an unavoidable tip-broadening effect of the AFM imaging, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> which, however, does not influence the calculations of volume change rate, considering the sizes of Sn and the tip radius (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). At the end of the sodiation process, Sn particle reaches a volume expansion of 427%, which is close to the theoretical volume change (420%) when it is fully sodiated to Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>. The Sn nanoparticle maintained an irreversible volume expansion of 207% after desodiation. To deduct the contribution of SEI to the volume change, force spectroscopy is applied to provide quantitative measurements on the SEI thickness covered on the nanoparticle. Principles of detecting SEI thickness using force curve method of AFM have been reported in previous researches. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Representative forceâdisplacement curve (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8a</a>) was taken from the desodiated Sn nanoparticle. The force started to increase when the AFM probe tip contacted with the soft SEI. Upon interacting with the hard Sn particle, the force started to increase linearly. The distance between the fitted hard interaction distance and the surface of SEI was calculated to be the thickness of SEI. The distribution of SEI thickness (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8b</a>) covered on the particle is in a range of 0.5â4.5 nm and the average value is 2.13 nm. Compared with the particle size of Sn (180 nm), the contribution of SEI to the volume expansion is relatively small (â¼2.3%) and could be ignored. This phenomenon of irreversible volume change is in agreement with the observation on other alloy-type anodes by using in situ TEM. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51 ref52 ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51 ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 51 to 53">(51-53)</a> It is probably caused by the following reasons: (1) nanoscale pores formed inside the particles during the dealloying process; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51 ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51 ref54" aria-label="reference 51 and 54">(51, 54)</a> and (2) the diffusion-controlled trapping of alkali metal in Sn particles, which causes limited dealloying and thus contributes to partial irreversible volume change. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The volume change of Sn nanoparticles with various sizes (80, 100, 120, 180, 200, 230, 260, and 330 nm) was characterized after initial sodiation and desodiation. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a> shows the relationship between the volume change and the pristine particle size. The corresponding 3D AFM images and the cross-section analysis of particles are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S9 and S10</a>, respectively. Regardless of the particle size, all of the Sn nanoparticles swell dramatically after being fully sodiated, with similar expansion ratios close to the theoretical value (420%). However, after desodiation, the irreversible volume change exhibits a continuously ascending tendency with an increase in the particle size. The 80 nm Sn shows the lowest irreversible change rate of 190%. For the particles with sizes 100, 120, and 200 nm, the volume contracts to 200, 202, and 233%, respectively. The Sn nanoparticles above 200 nm bear much larger irreversible volume change. The irreversible volume of 230, 260, and 330 nm particles increases to 278, 288, and 320%, respectively. As no separation of all of Sn particles from Au film was observed (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>), these irreversible volume changes do not result from the electrical disconnection between particles and current collector. Therefore, decreasing the particle size significantly improves the volume recovery of Sn nanoparticle on first desodiation, which may exert a profound effect on the cycling performance in the following cycles.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/medium/am-2017-08870h_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/large/am-2017-08870h_0001.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. In situ AFM measurements of a series of Sn nanoparticle during sodiation and desodiation in the first cycle. Volume change of Sn nanoparticles of 80, 100, 120, 200, 230, 260, and 330 nm after sodiation and desodiation.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/large/am-2017-08870h_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b08870&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphological evolution of representative small (70 nm) and large (240 nm) Sn particles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>) was further investigated during the first five cycles. Particles below 70 nm had also been tested, whereas the coverage of byproducts and the continuous scanning of probe influenced the normal testing of small particles below 70 nm. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a,b shows the 3D images and the corresponding volume change of the 70 nm Sn particle. There is apparent contrast on the right of the particle during electrochemical testing, which might originate from the byproducts of the decomposition of the electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The first sodiation results in a volume expansion of 422%, which returns to 190% after desodiation. Similar volume changes are found in the next four cycles. The average thickness of SEI (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12a</a>) increased from 1.90 to 3 nm during the cycles, and the contribution of SEI to the irreversible volume variation changed slightly from 5.6 to 8.5%. In addition, the particle shape remains stable, indicating that the irreversible volume change might originate from the nanoscale pores formed in the previous desodiation and partially accommodate the expansion in the following sodiation process. Similar phenomenon has also been reported in the case of depotassiation from Sn. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> For the larger particle (240 nm), the mechanical stability is largely declined after several cycles of discharge and charge. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c,d, the volume expansion is 430% in the first cycle and continuously increases subsequently. After the fifth sodiation process, the volume change rate reaches as high as 520%, and the shape of the particle starts to change. The thickness of SEI and its contribution to the volume also keeps increasing slightly during the five cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12b</a>). The unstable shape maintenance of the larger Sn nanoparticle indicates that more trapped sodium or more nanovoids have been formed in the structure. This continuous morphology change of a larger Sn particle would cause a structure fracture and worsen the electrochemical performance in real batteries.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/medium/am-2017-08870h_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/large/am-2017-08870h_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. In situ AFM measurements of selected Sn nanoparticles during sodiation and desodiation in cycles. Three-dimensional AFM images and the corresponding volume change of particles of 70 (a, b) and 240 nm (c, d).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/large/am-2017-08870h_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b08870&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical measurement of coin-type half cells was also carried out to verify the difference in the cycling performance between Sn nanoparticles with large and small sizes. The Sn nanoparticles tested here are synthesized by a surfactant-assisted method (details of preparation are described in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). The synthesized Sn nanoparticles show a high phase purity (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>) and a homogeneous morphology with mean sizes of 75 and 260 nm (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a,b), respectively. The galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c,d) and the cycling performance (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e,f) of these two Sn materials exhibit distinct trends in the first five cycles. The 75 nm Sn nanoparticles deliver higher capacity with a better capacity retention, which is due to the better shape maintenance as observed by the AFM. However, the larger Sn shows a faster fading capacity and a lower Coulombic efficiency. Therefore, it can be concluded that the mechanical stability and cyclability of Sn can be improved significantly by merely downsizing. Confining ultrasmall Sn in an elastic matrix can be expected to further enhance the electrode performance.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/medium/am-2017-08870h_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/large/am-2017-08870h_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. SEM images, particle size distribution histogram (inset), galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles (in the potential window of 0.01â2 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), and cycling performance of synthesized Sn nanoparticles of 75 nm (a, c, e) and 260 nm (b, d, f). Scale bar: 100 nm.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/asset/images/large/am-2017-08870h_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b08870&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we have used AFM to analyze the sodiation and desodiation processes of single Sn nanoparticle directly in liquid electrolyte. Our results indicate that the volume expansion of Sn nanoparticles is always â¼420% after the first full sodiation, whereas the volume change in desodiation is strongly size dependent. Interestingly, compared with the larger Sn nanoparticles, the smaller ones show a lower irreversible volume change rate and a better shape maintenance after the desodiation process. Decreasing the particle size significantly enhances the mechanical stability and thus the cycling performance of Sn nanoparticles. This finding provides new insights into what is happening to Sn anode on charging and discharging. A mechanistic understanding of the size-dependent behavior also indicates the importance of size and interface controlling in the design of alloy-based electrode materials undergoing large volume variation during cycling.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.7b08870" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.7b08870</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Detailed experimental procedures and additional materials characterization (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf">am7b08870_si_001.pdf (2.01 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b08870" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have been proposed as candidates for replacing Li-ion batteries.  The authors examine the viability of Na-ion anode materials based on Na alloys or hard carbons in terms of volumetric energy d.  Due to the bigger size of the Na atom compared to the Li atom, Na alloys would lead to anode materials with about half the volumetric energy d. of their Li analogs.  Volumetric energy densities obtainable with sodiated hard carbons would also be significantly less than those obtainable with lithiated graphite.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tin and Tin Compounds for Sodium Ion Battery Anodes: Phase Transformations and Performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Zhi; Ding, Jia; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">48</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1657-1665</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium ion batteries (NIB, NAB, SIB) are attracting interest as a potentially lower cost alternative to lithium ion batteries (LIB), with readily available and geog. democratic reserves of the metal.  Tin is one of most promising SIB anode materials, which alloys with up to 3.75 Na, leading to a charge storage capacity of 847 mAh g-1.  In this Account, we outline the state-of-the-art understanding regarding the sodiation-induced phase transformations and the assocd. performance in a range of Sn-based systems, treating metallic Sn and its alloys, tin oxide (SnO2), tin sulfide (SnS2/SnS), and tin phosphide (Sn4P3).  We first detail what is known about the sodiation sequence in metallic Sn, highlighting the most recent insight into the reactions prior to the terminal equil. Na15Sn4 intermetallic.  We explain why researchers argue that the equil. (phase diagram) series of phase transitions does not occur in this system, and rather why sodiation/desodiation proceeds through a series of metastable cryst. and amorphous structures.  We also outline the recent modeling-based insight regarding how this phase transition profoundly influences the mech. properties of the alloy, progressively changing the bonding and the near neighbor arrangement from "Sn-like" to "Na-like" in the process.  We then go on to discuss the sodiation reactions in SnO2.  We argue that while a substantial amt. of exptl. work already exists where the focus is on synthesis and testing of tin oxide-based nanocomposites, the exact sodiation sequence is just beginning to be understood.  Unlike in Sn and Sn alloys, where capacities near the theor. are reached at least early during cycling, SnO2 never quite achieves anything close to the 1398 mAh g-1 that would be possible with a combination of fully reversible conversion and alloying reactions.  We highlight recent work demonstrating that contrary to general expectations, it is the Sn to Na15Sn4 alloying reaction that is incomplete and hence limits the capacity of the electrode.  We also describe how the oxide conversion reaction goes through an intermediate SnO phase, and how its reversibility in a half-cell is highly dependent on the terminal anodic voltage.  We then present what is known about sodiation of tin sulfide and of tin phosphide phases, including emerging microstructural evidence that may explain why both the sulfides and the phosphides are unable to achieve their highly promising theor. capacities under conventional electrode testing conditions.  Finally, we provide a broad comparison of the capacity (cycling and rate) performance for a range of Sn based anode materials, and show that there may be indeed an optimum microstructural architecture.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGraHWeuL6zqAbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li9qZuQc7jTdw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtVakurzM&amp;md5=7305836f3a3bbf9d5e81e811cd8ab069</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201603212"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Rouse, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Foix, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dugas, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Alves Dalla Corte, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tarascon, J.-M.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Microsized Sn as Advanced Anodes in Glyme-Based Electrolyte for Na-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Universal quinone electrodes for long cycle life aqueous rechargeable batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liang, Yanliang; Jing, Yan; Gheytani, Saman; Lee, Kuan-Yi; Liu, Ping; Facchetti, Antonio; Yao, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Aq. rechargeable batteries provide the safety, robustness, affordability, and environmental friendliness necessary for grid storage and elec. vehicle operations, but their adoption is plagued by poor cycle life due to the structural and chem. instability of the anode materials. Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">27</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">6702</span><span class="NLM_x">â</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">6707</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tin Nanodots Encapsulated in Porous Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Nanofibers as a Free-Standing Anode for Advanced Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yongchang; Zhang, Ning; Jiao, Lifang; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">27</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">42</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6702-6707</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0935-9648</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Ultra-small Sn nanodots (1-2 nm) were delicately encapsulated in porous N-doped carbon nanofibers through electrospinning and thermal treatment.  The size and content of Sn particles can be controlled by changing reaction conditions.  When evaluated as binder- and current collector-free anodes for rechargeable Na-ion batteries, Sn NDs@PNC nanofibers delivered a reversible capacity of 633 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1.  More impressively, a capacity of 450 mA h g-1 even at 10 000 mA g-1 and 483 mA h g-1 over 1300 cycles at 2000 mA g-1 were achieved.  To our knowledge, this is the highest rate performance and cyclic stability of tin for Na storage.  The exceptional electrochem. performance of Sn NDs@PNC is attributed to the following: First, the ultra-small Sn NDs (1-2 nm) decreases the abs. strain and improve the utilization rate of active materials during sodiation/de-sodiation process; second, the uniform distribution of Sn NDs in the fibrous carbon matrix is an important factor to generate a balanced stress over the whole composite with maintaining the integrity of the electrode upon prolonged cycling; third, the 1D porous N-doped carbon frame can interlink into a 3D conductive network, which facilitates the electron and Na+ diffusion kinetics and buffers the large vol. fluctuation of Sn NDs and prevents their pulverization and aggregation.  Carbon fibers also serve as both current collector and conductive material - this avoids the common loss of electrochem. contact between the active materials and the current collector/conductive additives, and reduces the inactive wt. and vol. of cells thus improving the energy d.  This simple and scalable synthesis as well as the promising electrochem. performance encourages application of Sn-based materials as anodes for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqnXAQ9MCRr0LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li9qZuQc7jTdw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsF2ks77J&amp;md5=c6f2a6fad6ab6e844a9149e974f0edf1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b13830"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gu, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dou, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chou, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dou, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Multiangular Rod-Shaped Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> as Cathode Materials with High Rate and Long Life for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Template-Free Electrochemical Synthesis of Sn Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode Materials for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nam, Do-Hwan; Kim, Tae-Hee; Hong, Kyung-Sik; Kwon, Hyuk-Sang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sn nanofibers with a high aspect ratio are successfully synthesized using a simple electrodeposition process from an aq. soln. without the use of templates.  The synthesis approach involves the rapid electrochem. deposition of Sn accompanied by the strong adsorption of Triton X-100, which can function as a growth modifier for the Sn crystallites.  Triton X-100 is adsorbed on the {200} crystallog. planes of Sn in an elongated configuration and suppressed the preferential growth of Sn along the [100] direction.  Consequently, the Sn electrodeposits are forced to grow anisotropically in a direction normal to the (112) or (â¾112) plane, forming one-dimensional nanofibers.  As electrode materials for the Na-ion batteries, the Sn nanofibers exhibit a high reversible capacity and an excellent cycle performance; the charge capacity is maintained at 776.26 mA-h/g after 100 cycles, which corresponds to a retention of 95.09% of the initial charge capacity.  The superior electrochem. performance of the Sn nanofibers is mainly attributed to the high mech. stability of the nanofibers, which originate from highly anisotropic expansion during sodiation and the pore vols. existing between the nanofibers.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpMet3lkd_Y6bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljxInNMdmCuyg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvVegu7rN&amp;md5=76f47655b744f6cccb1ae0ba7a7760d4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201600811"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Park, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kang, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Developments of the Lithium Metal Anode for Rechargeable Non-Aqueous Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Combination of Lightweight Elements and Nanostructured Materials for Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Jun; Cheng, Fangyi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of progress in battery development using a combination of lightwt. elements and nanostructured materials.  Nanostructures offer advantages over comparable bulk materials in improving battery performance.  We highlight the benefits of nanostructured active materials for primary zinc-manganese dioxide, lithium-manganese dioxide, and metal-air batteries, as well as rechargeable lithium-ion and nickel-metal hydride batteries.  Through selected examples, we illustrate the effect of structure, shape, and size on the electrochem. properties of electrode materials.  Because of their numerous active sites and facile electronic/ionic transfer and diffusion, nanostructures can improve battery efficiency.  In particular, we demonstrate the properties of nanostructured active materials including Mg, Al, Si, Zn, MnO2, CuV2O6, LiNi0.8Co0.2O2, LiFePO4, Fe2O3, Co3O4, TiS2, and Ni(OH)2 in battery applications.  Electrochem. investigations reveal that we generally attain larger capacities and improved kinetics for electrode materials as their av. particle size decreases.  Novel nanostructures such as nanowires, nanotubes, nanourchins, and porous nanospheres show lower activation energy, enhanced reactivity, improved high-rate charge/discharge capability, and more controlled structural flexibility than their bulk counterparts.  In particular, anode materials such as Si nanospheres and Fe2O3 nanotubes can deliver reversible capacity exceeding 500 mAÂ·h/g.  Nanocomposite cathode materials such as NiP-doped LiFePO4 and metal hydroxide-coated Ni(OH)2 nanotubes allow us to integrate functional components, which enhance elec. cond. and suppress vol. expansion.  Therefore, shifting from bulk to nanostructured electrode materials could offer a revolutionary opportunity to develop advanced green batteries with large capacity, high energy and power d., and long cycle life.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpIYGAR_Y_SprVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liXqhSv4ZMlBQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1MXktF2gt70%253D&amp;md5=ed7edff1f5d424cdd554631ae71ce2c1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1039/c1ee01592c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, L.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Uniform Nano-Sn/C Composite Anodes for Lithium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Yunhua; Liu, Qing; Zhu, Yujie; Liu, Yihang; Langrock, Alex; Zachariah, Michael R.; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
