        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nano-Sn/C composites are ideal anode materials for high energy and power d. Li-ion batteries.  However, because of the low m.p. of Sn and the tendency of grain growth, esp. during high temp. carbonization, it was a significant challenge to create well-dispersed ultra-small Sn nanoparticles within a C matrix.  The authors demonstrate an aerosol spray pyrolysis technique, as a facile and scalable method, to synthesize a nano-Sn/C composite with uniformly dispersed 10 nm nano-Sn within a spherical C matrix.  The discharge capacity of nano-Sn/C composite sphere anodes maintains the initial capacity of 710 mA-h/g after 130 cycles at 0.25 C.  The nano-Sn/C composite sphere anodes can provide â¼600 mA-h/g even at a high rate of 20 C.  Such high rate performance for Sn anodes was not reported previously.  The exceptional performance of the nano-Sn/C composite is attributed to the unique nano-Sn/C structure: (a) C matrix offers mech. support to accommodate the stress assocd. with the large vol. change of nano-Sn, thus alleviating pulverization; (b) the C matrix prevents Sn nanoparticle agglomeration upon prolonged cycling; and (c) C network provides continuous path for Li ions and electrons inside the nano-Sn/C composite spheres.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrLYyFaDGc2HbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liXqhSv4ZMlBQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhslGgtw%253D%253D&amp;md5=eb1dcc0d9d76d861eb8b2a90655d874b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1021/am4023994"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lin, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Abel, P. Peter; Kupsta, Martin; Zahiri, Beniamin; Kohandehghan, Alireza; Lotfabad, Elmira Memarzadeh; Li, Zhi; Luber, Erik J.; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Here we provide the first report on several compns. of ternary Sn-Ge-Sb thin film alloys for application as sodium ion battery (aka NIB, NaB or SIB) anodes, employing Sn50Ge50, Sb50Ge50, and pure Sn, Ge, Sb as baselines. HRTEM shows that Sn50Ge25Sb25 is a composite of 10-15 nm Sn and Sn-alloyed Ge nanocrystallites that are densely dispersed within an amorphous matrix.  Comparing the microstructures of alloys where the capacity significantly exceeds the rule of mixts. prediction to those where it does not leads us to hypothesize that this new phenomenon originates from the Ge(Sn) that is able to sodiate beyond the 1:1 Na:Ge ratio reported for the pure element.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries offer an attractive option for low cost grid scale storage due to the abundance of Na.  Sn is touted as a high capacity anode for Na-ion batteries with a high theor. capacity of 847 mA-h/g, but it has several limitations such as large vol. expansion with cycling, slow kinetics, and unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation.  An anode consisting of a Sn thin film deposited on a hierarchical wood fiber substrate simultaneously addresses all the challenges assocd. with Sn anodes.  The soft nature of wood fibers effectively releases the mech. stresses assocd. with the sodiation process, and the mesoporous structure functions as an electrolyte reservoir that allows for ion transport through the outer and inner surface of the fiber.  These properties are confirmed exptl. and computationally.  A stable cycling performance of 400 cycles with an initial capacity of 339 mA-h/g is demonstrated; a significant improvement over other reported Sn nanostructures.  The soft and mesoporous wood fiber substrate can be used as a new platform for low cost Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrPjnqhdJ5q7rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhk2GKsNPeV3Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXosVegtr0%253D&amp;md5=b49c25ccb079701ddb3c8a22d15156ad</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200346"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Performance of Porous Carbon/Tin Composite Anodes for Sodium-Ion and Lithium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. performance of mesoporous carbon (C)/tin (Sn) anodes in Na-ion and Li-ion batteries is systematically investigated.  The mesoporous C/Sn anodes in a Na-ion battery shows similar cycling stability but lower capacity and poorer rate capability than that in a Li-ion battery.  The desodiation potentials of Sn anodes are approx. 0.21 V lower than delithiation potentials.  The low capacity and poor rate capability of C/Sn anode in Na-ion batteries is mainly due to the large Na-ion size, resulting in slow Na-ion diffusion and large vol. change of porous C/Sn composite anode during alloy/dealloy reactions. To minimize the ecological implications associated with their wider use, we must integrate sustainability of battery materials into our research endeavours, choosing chemistries that have a minimum footprint in nature and that are more readily recycled or integrated into a full circular economy.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Materials' Methods: NMR in Battery Research</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pecher, Oliver; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Griffith, Kent J.; Grey, Clare P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review is presented.  Improving electrochem. energy storage is one of the major issues of our time.  The search for new battery materials together with the drive to improve performance and lower cost of existing and new batteries is not without its challenges.  Success in these matters is undoubtedly based on first understanding the underlying chemistries of the materials and the relations between the components involved.  A combined application of exptl. and theor. techniques has proven to be a powerful strategy to gain insights into many of the questions that arise from the 'how do batteries work and why do they fail' challenge.  In this special issue we highlight the application of solid-state NMR (NMR) spectroscopy in battery research - a technique that can be extremely powerful in characterizing local structures in battery materials, even in highly disordered systems.  An introduction on electrochem. energy storage illustrates the research aims and prospective approaches to reach these.  We particularly address 'NMR in battery research' by giving a brief introduction to electrochem. techniques and applications as well as background information on both in and ex situ solid-state NMR spectroscopy.  We will try to answer the question 'Is NMR suitable and how can it help me to solve my problem' by shortly reviewing some of our recent research on electrodes, microstructure formation, electrolytes and interfaces, in which the application of NMR was helpful.  Finally, we share hands-on experience directly from the lab bench to answer the fundamental question 'Where and how should I start' to help guide a researcher's way through the manifold approaches possible.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqSktNdpE_2crVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhIvy5UwP64IA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhslCkt7jM&amp;md5=d150b75264cb232248a15ba33c102dd5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1038/nmat4577"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shpigel, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Levi, M.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In situ hydrodynamic spectroscopy for structure characterization of porous energy storage electrodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shpigel, Netanel; Levi, Mikhael D.; Sigalov, Sergey; Girshevitz, Olga; Aurbach, Doron; Daikhin, Leonid; Pikma, Piret; Marandi, Margus; Janes, Alar; Lust, Enn; Jackel, Nicolas; Presser, Volker</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A primary at.-scale effect accompanying Li-ion insertion into rechargeable battery electrodes is a significant intercalation-induced change of the unit cell vol. of the cryst. material.  This generates a variety of secondary multi-scale dimensional changes and causes a deterioration in the energy storage performance stability.  Although traditional in situ height-sensing techniques (at. force microscopy or electrochem. dilatometry) are able to sense electrode thickness changes at a nanometer scale, they are much less informative concerning intercalation-induced changes of the porous electrode structure at a mesoscopic scale.  Based on a electrochem. quartz-crystal microbalance with dissipation monitoring on multiple overtone orders, herein the authors introduce an in situ hydrodynamic spectroscopic method for porous electrode structure characterization.  This new method will enable future developments and applications in the fields of battery and supercapacitor research, esp. for diagnostics of viscoelastic properties of binders for composite electrodes and probing the micromech. stability of their internal electrode porous structure and interfaces.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpyvJhCwizB8bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhIvy5UwP64IA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XktVamsL0%253D&amp;md5=2d0cf6ab99a62b4cacd89ef8f3ecf059</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.5b09553"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">ArÃ¡n-Ais, R.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We have employed identical location transmission electron microscopy (IL-TEM) to study changes in the shape and morphol. of faceted Pt nanoparticles as a result of electrochem. cycling; a procedure typically employed for activating platinum surfaces.  We find that the shape and morphol. of the as-prepd. hexagonal nanoparticles are rapidly degraded as a result of potential cycling up to +1.3 V.         </div><div class="casAbstract">To meet future needs for industries from personal devices to automobiles, state-of-the-art rechargeable lithium-ion batteries will require both improved durability and lowered costs.  To enhance battery performance and lifetime, understanding electrode degrdn. mechanisms is of crit. importance.  Various advanced in situ and operando characterization tools developed during the past few years have proven indispensable for optimizing battery materials, understanding cell degrdn. mechanisms, and ultimately improving the overall battery performance.  Here we review recent progress in the development and application of advanced characterization techniques such as in situ transmission electron microscopy for high-performance lithium-ion batteries.  Using three representative electrode systems-layered metal oxides, Li-rich layered oxides and Si-based or Sn-based alloys-we discuss how these tools help researchers understand the battery process and design better battery systems.                         cas_cit31R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">31</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structural Evolution and Pulverization of Tin Nanoparticles during Lithiation-Delithiation Cycling</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Jiangwei; Fan, Feifei; Liu, Yang; Jungjohann, Katherine L.; Lee, Seung Woo; Mao, Scott X.; Liu, Xiaohua; Zhu, Ting</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Pulverization is a major cause of the capacity fade and poor cyclability of Sn-based anodes in Li-ion batteries.  The authors study the structural evolution of Sn nanoparticles during electrochem. lithiation-delithiation cycling by in situ TEM.  The Î²-Sn nanoparticles in the size range of 79-526 nm are lithiated to the crystal Li22Sn5 phase via a 2-step mechanism, and no cracking or fracture is obsd., distinct from the lithiation-induced fracture in micron-sized Sn particles.  Lithiation can induce the aggregation of small Sn nanoparticles with diam. in tens of nanometers, while delithiation can lead to the pulverization of large Sn nanoparticles.  Similarly, the aggregation and pulverization of Sn nanoparticles are also obsd. during sodiation and desodiation, resp.  The delithiation/desodiation induced pulverization of Sn nanoparticles is attributed to the high dealloying rate that results in cracks and voids in dealloyed Sn anodes.  Based on the in situ TEM results, a size-dependent pulverization mechanism of Sn particles is proposed.  The work advances the mechanistic understanding of the pulverization and degrdn. mechanisms of Sn particle-based electrodes in Li-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrg_MxNGu_DB7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li5QTcrwL3atQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhslahsLnP&amp;md5=079293681cb24545a2ac3f35d2709380</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit32" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2011.08.026"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lucas, I. The workstation includes an evacuable glovebox that maintains a pure atm. in which the microscopes are located, thus enabling measurements in a highly pure argon atm.  The system is based on a compact and functional evacuable glovebox which is placed in a special construction which provides full protection against vibrations.  This was obtained by suspending the box by flexible cords during the expts., while all the piping connections are removed.  The concept of an evacuable glovebox, which can be back-filled by a pure atm., enables measurements to be performed under a pure inert atm., eliminating the need for noisy gas purifn. systems.  Pure solns. and highly reactive electrode materials are introduced into this glovebox using a transfer method, based on a hermetically sealed transfer vessel and a special vacuum chamber in the glovebox.  Preliminary results demonstrated that high-quality imaging of reactive electrochem. systems can be obtained using this system.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Using electron beam lithog., amorphous Si (a-Si) nanopillars were fabricated with a height of 100 nm and diams. of 100, 200, 300, 500, and 1000 nm. In situ at. force microscopy (AFM) was used to qual. and quant. examine the morphol. evolution of the nanopillars including vol. and height changes vs. voltage in real-time. During subsequent cycles the a-Si roughened and deformed from the initial geometry, and eventually pillars with diams. &gt;200 nm fractured.  Furthermore, a degrdn. of mech. properties is suggested as the 100 and 200 nm pillars were mech. eroded by the small contact forces under the AFM probe.  Ex situ SEM (SEM) images, combined with anal. of the damage caused by in situ AFM imaging, demonstrate that during cycling, the silicon became porous and structurally unstable compared to as-fabricated pillars.                         cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Insight into the Interfacial Process and Mechanism in Lithium-Sulfur Batteries: An In Situ AFM Study</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lang, Shuang-Yan; Shi, Yang; Guo, Yu-Guo; Wang, Dong; Wen, Rui; Wan, Li-Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">51</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-sulfur (Li-S) batteries are highly appealing for large-scale energy storage.  However, performance deterioration issues remain, which are highly related to interfacial properties.  Herein, we present a direct visualization of the interfacial structure and dynamics of the Li-S discharge/charge processes at the nanoscale.  In situ at. force microscopy and ex situ spectroscopic methods directly distinguish the morphol. and growth processes of insol. products Li2S2 and Li2S.  The monitored interfacial dynamics show that Li2S2 nanoparticle nuclei begin to grow at 2 V followed by a fast deposition of lamellar Li2S at 1.83 V on discharge.  Upon charging, only Li2S depletes from the interface, leaving some Li2S2 undissolved, which accumulates during cycling.  The galvanostatic pptn. of Li2S2 and/or Li2S is correlated to current rates and affects the specific capacity.  These findings reveal a straightforward structure-reactivity correlation and performance fading mechanism in Li-S batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoja949rk5OxLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljk23iUE6LEYw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhvVynsr3O&amp;md5=5d4fe0b1bf6fd1d31c329490da094bef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1021/am500363t"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tokranov, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sheldon, B.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Precise in situ at. force microscopy (AFM) is used to monitor the formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on Si electrodes.  The stability of these passivation films on neg. electrodes is critically important in rechargeable Li-ion batteries, and high capacity materials such as Si present substantial challenges because of the large vol. changes that occur with Li insertion and removal.  The results reported here show that the initial rapid SEI formation can be stabilized before significant Li insertion into the Si begins and that the rate at which this occurs varies significantly with the nature of the surface.  The initial cycling conditions also have a substantial impact on the SEI that forms, with faster rates leading to a smoother, thinner SEI film.  To quant. interpret the SEI measurements, irreversible expansion of the Si during the first cycle was also monitored in situ with specifically designed specimen configurations.  On the basis of the exptl. results, relatively simple models were also used to describe the initial formation and stabilization of the SEI and to describe the relationship between the SEI thickness and expected SEI degrdn. mechanisms.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrmiAW86wovhbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljk23iUE6LEYw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXkvVyrtbk%253D&amp;md5=a9a7fd0b1f20cd0aac7b3d9416b67517</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit44" data-doi="10.1021/am505847s"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, X.-R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Deng, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yan, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guo, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wan, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Single Nanowire Electrode Electrochemistry of Silicon Anode by In Situ Atomic Force Microscopy: Solid Electrolyte Interphase Growth and Mechanical Properties</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.                         cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Influences of Additives on the Formation of a Solid Electrolyte Interphase on MnO Electrode Studied by Atomic Force Microscopy and Force Spectroscopy</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Jie; Yang, Xiaocheng; Wang, Rui; Dong, Weiling; Lu, Wei; Wu, Xiaodong; Wang, Xiaoping; Li, Hong; Chen, Liwei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) that forms on electrodes largely defines the performances of Li ion batteries (LIBs), such as cycling performance, shelf life, and safety.  Additives in the electrolyte can modify the properties of the SEI and thus efficiently improve the performances of LIBs.  However, the effects of additives on the mech. properties, structure, and stability of the SEI have rarely been studied directly.  The authors report the influence of vinylene carbonate (VC) and lithium bis(oxalate)borate (LiBOB) additives on the mech. properties of SEI films formed on MnO electrodes using at. force microscopy (AFM) and force spectroscopy.  The SEI formed from VC additive is thick and soft and partially decomps. upon charging.  LiBOB forms thin, stiff, and electrochem. stable SEI films, but the stiff SEI may not be favorable for adapting the vol. change of the electrodes.  The VC and LiBOB mixed additive combines the advantages of the 2 components and produces stable SEI with moderate thickness and stiffness.  Also the AFM-force spectroscopy method is effective in studying the structure and mech. properties of SEI films.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr3V8LrCQKYZ7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljy7_AdgxsnfA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhtlGmsb7O&amp;md5=6f2228087217cddcad74f9c34b714500</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit46" data-doi="10.1039/C4CP01968G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zheng, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zheng, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ben, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">3D Visualization of Inhomogeneous Multi-Layered Structure and Youngâs Modulus of the Solid Electrolyte Interphase (SEI) on Silicon Anodes for Lithium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Phys.                         cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanovoid formation and annihilation in gallium nanodroplets under lithiation-delithiation cycling</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liang, Wentao; Hong, Liang; Yang, Hui; Fan, FeiFei; Liu, Yang; Li, Hong; Li, Ju; Huang, Jian Yu; Chen, Long-Qing; Zhu, Ting; Zhang, Sulin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

Here we report the self-healing behavior of gallium nanodroplets (GaNDs) under electrochem. cycling at room temp., obsd. with in situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM).                 10.1021/nl3044508</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D13%26publication_year%3D2013%26pages%3D758-764%26journal%3DNano%2BLett.%26author%3DM.%2BT.%2BMcDowell%26author%3DS.%2BW.%2BLee%26author%3DJ.%2BT.%2BHarris%26author%3DB.%2BA.%2BKorgel%26author%3DC.%2BWang%26author%3DW.%2BD.%2BNix%26author%3DY.%2BCui%26title%3DIn%2BSitu%2BTEM%2Bof%2BTwo-Phase%2BLithiation%2Bof%2BAmorphous%2BSilicon%2BNanospheres%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fnl3044508&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b08870&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fnl3044508&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In Situ TEM of Two-Phase Lithiation of Amorphous Silicon Nanospheres</span></div><div class="casAuthors">McDowell, Matthew T.; Lee, Seok Woo; Harris, Justin T.; Korgel, Brian A.; Wang, Chongmin; Nix, William D.; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">758-764</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">To utilize high-capacity Si anodes in next-generation Li-ion batteries, the phys. and chem. transformations during the Li-Si reaction must be better understood.  Here, in situ transmission electron microscopy is used to observe the lithiation/delithiation of amorphous Si nanospheres; amorphous Si is an important anode material that has been less studied than cryst. Si.  Unexpectedly, the expts. reveal that the first lithiation occurs via a two-phase mechanism, which is contrary to previous understanding and has important consequences for mech. stress evolution during lithiation.  On the basis of kinetics measurements, this behavior is suggested to be due to the rate-limiting effect of Si-Si bond breaking.  In addn., the results show that amorphous Si has more favorable kinetics and fracture behavior when reacting with Li than does cryst. Si, making it advantageous to use in battery electrodes.  Amorphous spheres up to 870 nm in diam. do not fracture upon lithiation; this is much larger than the 150 nm crit. fracture diam. previously identified for cryst. Si spheres.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqm0_-iJ381vLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ligp822C2JcPQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXoslOisg%253D%253D&amp;md5=277b7d29cf8619f32d381cb155130da6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit54" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpcc.7b03837"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ni, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tian, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mao, S.                 10.1021/acs.jpcc.7b03837</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D121%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D12652-12657%26journal%3DJ.%2BPhys.%2BChem.%2BC%26author%3DQ.%2BWang%26author%3DX.%2BZhao%26author%3DC.%2BNi%26author%3DH.%2BTian%26author%3DJ.%2BLi%26author%3DZ.%2BZhang%26author%3DS.%2BX.%2BMao%26author%3DJ.%2BWang%26author%3DY.%2BXu%26title%3DReaction%2Band%2BCapacity-Fading%2BMechanisms%2Bof%2BTin%2BNanoparticles%2Bin%2BPotassium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.jpcc.7b03837&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b08870&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.jpcc.7b03837&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Reaction and Capacity-Fading Mechanisms of Tin Nanoparticles in Potassium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Qiannan; Zhao, Xinxin; Ni, Chaolun; Tian, He; Li, Jixue; Zhang, Ze; Mao, Scott X.; Wang, Jiangwei; Xu, Yunhua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">121</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">23</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">12652-12657</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCCCK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1932-7447</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors report a study of the electrochem. performance and degrdn. mechanism of Sn nanoparticle anodes in K-ion batteries (KIBs).  A high capacity of 197 mA h/g was found for the Sn nanoparticles in KIBs.  In situ TEM characterization revealed a 2-step potassiation mechanism: formation of a KSn phase after full potassiation and reversible nanopore formation during the cycling of Sn nanoparticles.  However, significant capacity fading occurred after a few cycles, which was caused by the severe pulverization of the Sn nanoparticles.  This work offers a fundamental understanding of the reaction and degrdn. mechanisms of alloying-type anodes for KIBs, shedding light on the development of high-performance KIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrcEkfcho14bLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ligp822C2JcPQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXnvFCns7g%253D&amp;md5=d87825c7d37151ee62b017cff0c9e7e7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit55" data-doi="10.1039/C7EE00244K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Rehnlund, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lindgren, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">BÃ¶hme, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nordh, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zou, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pettersson, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bexell, U.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Boman, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">EdstrÃ¶n, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nyholm, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lithium Trapping in Alloy Forming Electrodes and Current Collectors for Lithium Based Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Energy Environ. Sci.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">10</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1350</span><span class="NLM_x">â</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">1357</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C7EE00244K</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D10%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D1350-1357%26journal%3DEnergy%2BEnviron.%2BSci.%26author%3DD.%2BRehnlund%26author%3DF.%2BLindgren%26author%3DS.%2BB%25C3%25B6hme%26author%3DT.%2BNordh%26author%3DY.%2BZou%26author%3DJ.%2BPettersson%26author%3DU.%2BBexell%26author%3DM.%2BBoman%26author%3DK.%2BEdstr%25C3%25B6n%26author%3DL.%2BNyholm%26title%3DLithium%2BTrapping%2Bin%2BAlloy%2BForming%2BElectrodes%2Band%2BCurrent%2BCollectors%2Bfor%2BLithium%2BBased%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC7EE00244K&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b08870&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC7EE00244K&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium trapping in alloy forming electrodes and current collectors for lithium based batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Rehnlund, David; Lindgren, Fredrik; Boehme, Solveig; Nordh, Tim; Zou, Yiming; Pettersson, Jean; Bexell, Ulf; Boman, Mats; Edstroem, Kristina; Nyholm, Leif</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1350-1357</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Significant capacity losses are generally seen for batteries contg. high-capacity lithium alloy forming anode materials such as silicon, tin and aluminum.  These losses are generally ascribed to a combination of vol. expansion effects and irreversible electrolyte redn. reactions.  Here, it is shown, based on e.g. elemental analyses of cycled electrodes, that the capacity losses for tin nanorod and silicon composite electrodes in fact involve diffusion controlled trapping of lithium in the electrodes.  While an analogous effect is also demonstrated for copper, nickel and titanium current collectors, boron-doped diamond is shown to function as an effective lithium diffusion barrier.  The present findings indicate that the durability of lithium based batteries can be improved significantly via proper electrode design or regeneration of the used electrodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrsfUO_jKIgwbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liyaJl99vsCtQ"> &gt;&gt; L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ceder, G.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Challenges for Na-ion Negative Electrodes</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Electrochem. Soc.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2011</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">158</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">A1011</span><span class="NLM_x">â</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">A1014</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1149/1.3607983</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Challenges for Na-ion Negative Electrodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chevrier, V. L.; Ceder, G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">158</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">A1011-A1014</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4651</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have been proposed as candidates for replacing Li-ion batteries.  The authors examine the viability of Na-ion anode materials based on Na alloys or hard carbons in terms of volumetric energy d.  Due to the bigger size of the Na atom compared to the Li atom, Na alloys would lead to anode materials with about half the volumetric energy d. of their Li analogs.  Volumetric energy densities obtainable with sodiated hard carbons would also be significantly less than those obtainable with lithiated graphite.  These findings highlight the need of novel ideas for Na-ion anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpfbyHmIC7uUrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhVdp5mRQoZ3Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXpt1Ont7c%253D&amp;md5=f132981922c6ce146def4cd54c721e2b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00114"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ding, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mitlin, D.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Tin and Tin Compounds for Sodium Ion Battery Anodes: Phase Transformations and Performance</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Acc.     <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1657-1665</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACHRE4</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0001-4842</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium ion batteries (NIB, NAB, SIB) are attracting interest as a potentially lower cost alternative to lithium ion batteries (LIB), with readily available and geog. democratic reserves of the metal.  Tin is one of most promising SIB anode materials, which alloys with up to 3.75 Na, leading to a charge storage capacity of 847 mAh g-1.  In this Account, we outline the state-of-the-art understanding regarding the sodiation-induced phase transformations and the assocd. performance in a range of Sn-based systems, treating metallic Sn and its alloys, tin oxide (SnO2), tin sulfide (SnS2/SnS), and tin phosphide (Sn4P3).  We first detail what is known about the sodiation sequence in metallic Sn, highlighting the most recent insight into the reactions prior to the terminal equil. Na15Sn4 intermetallic.  We explain why researchers argue that the equil. (phase diagram) series of phase transitions does not occur in this system, and rather why sodiation/desodiation proceeds through a series of metastable cryst. and amorphous structures.  We also outline the recent modeling-based insight regarding how this phase transition profoundly influences the mech. properties of the alloy, progressively changing the bonding and the near neighbor arrangement from "Sn-like" to "Na-like" in the process.  We then go on to discuss the sodiation reactions in SnO2.  We argue that while a substantial amt. of exptl. work already exists where the focus is on synthesis and testing of tin oxide-based nanocomposites, the exact sodiation sequence is just beginning to be understood.  Unlike in Sn and Sn alloys, where capacities near the theor. are reached at least early during cycling, SnO2 never quite achieves anything close to the 1398 mAh g-1 that would be possible with a combination of fully reversible conversion and alloying reactions.  We highlight recent work demonstrating that contrary to general expectations, it is the Sn to Na15Sn4 alloying reaction that is incomplete and hence limits the capacity of the electrode.  We also describe how the oxide conversion reaction goes through an intermediate SnO phase, and how its reversibility in a half-cell is highly dependent on the terminal anodic voltage.  We then present what is known about sodiation of tin sulfide and of tin phosphide phases, including emerging microstructural evidence that may explain why both the sulfides and the phosphides are unable to achieve their highly promising theor. capacities under conventional electrode testing conditions.  Finally, we provide a broad comparison of the capacity (cycling and rate) performance for a range of Sn based anode materials, and show that there may be indeed an optimum microstructural architecture.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGraHWeuL6zqAbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljgsYG247c9iQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtVakurzM&amp;md5=7305836f3a3bbf9d5e81e811cd8ab069</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201603212"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Rouse, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Foix, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dugas, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Alves Dalla Corte, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tarascon, J.-M.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Microsized Sn as Advanced Anodes in Glyme-Based Electrolyte for Na-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Universal quinone electrodes for long cycle life aqueous rechargeable batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liang, Yanliang; Jing, Yan; Gheytani, Saman; Lee, Kuan-Yi; Liu, Ping; Facchetti, Antonio; Yao, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>

                        rightTab-cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tin Nanodots Encapsulated in Porous Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Nanofibers as a Free-Standing Anode for Advanced Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yongchang; Zhang, Ning; Jiao, Lifang; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Ultra-small Sn nanodots (1-2 nm) were delicately encapsulated in porous N-doped carbon nanofibers through electrospinning and thermal treatment.  The size and content of Sn particles can be controlled by changing reaction conditions.  When evaluated as binder- and current collector-free anodes for rechargeable Na-ion batteries, Sn NDs@PNC nanofibers delivered a reversible capacity of 633 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1.  More impressively, a capacity of 450 mA h g-1 even at 10 000 mA g-1 and 483 mA h g-1 over 1300 cycles at 2000 mA g-1 were achieved.  To our knowledge, this is the highest rate performance and cyclic stability of tin for Na storage.  The exceptional electrochem. performance of Sn NDs@PNC is attributed to the following: First, the ultra-small Sn NDs (1-2 nm) decreases the abs. strain and improve the utilization rate of active materials during sodiation/de-sodiation process; second, the uniform distribution of Sn NDs in the fibrous carbon matrix is an important factor to generate a balanced stress over the whole composite with maintaining the integrity of the electrode upon prolonged cycling; third, the 1D porous N-doped carbon frame can interlink into a 3D conductive network, which facilitates the electron and Na+ diffusion kinetics and buffers the large vol. fluctuation of Sn NDs and prevents their pulverization and aggregation.  Carbon fibers also serve as both current collector and conductive material - this avoids the common loss of electrochem. contact between the active materials and the current collector/conductive additives, and reduces the inactive wt. and vol. of cells thus improving the energy d.  This simple and scalable synthesis as well as the promising electrochem. performance encourages application of Sn-based materials as anodes for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqnXAQ9MCRr0LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljgsYG247c9iQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsF2ks77J&amp;md5=c6f2a6fad6ab6e844a9149e974f0edf1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b13830"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gu, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dou, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chou, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dou, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Multiangular Rod-Shaped Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> as Cathode Materials with High Rate and Long Life for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl. Triton X-100 is adsorbed on the {200} crystallog. planes of Sn in an elongated configuration and suppressed the preferential growth of Sn along the [100] direction.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of progress in battery development using a combination of lightwt. elements and nanostructured materials.  Nanostructures offer advantages over comparable bulk materials in improving battery performance.  We highlight the benefits of nanostructured active materials for primary zinc-manganese dioxide, lithium-manganese dioxide, and metal-air batteries, as well as rechargeable lithium-ion and nickel-metal hydride batteries.  Through selected examples, we illustrate the effect of structure, shape, and size on the electrochem. properties of electrode materials.  Because of their numerous active sites and facile electronic/ionic transfer and diffusion, nanostructures can improve battery efficiency.  In particular, we demonstrate the properties of nanostructured active materials including Mg, Al, Si, Zn, MnO2, CuV2O6, LiNi0.8Co0.2O2, LiFePO4, Fe2O3, Co3O4, TiS2, and Ni(OH)2 in battery applications.  Electrochem. investigations reveal that we generally attain larger capacities and improved kinetics for electrode materials as their av. particle size decreases.  Novel nanostructures such as nanowires, nanotubes, nanourchins, and porous nanospheres show lower activation energy, enhanced reactivity, improved high-rate charge/discharge capability, and more controlled structural flexibility than their bulk counterparts.  In particular, anode materials such as Si nanospheres and Fe2O3 nanotubes can deliver reversible capacity exceeding 500 mAÂ·h/g.  Nanocomposite cathode materials such as NiP-doped LiFePO4 and metal hydroxide-coated Ni(OH)2 nanotubes allow us to integrate functional components, which enhance elec. cond. and suppress vol. expansion.  Therefore, shifting from bulk to nanostructured electrode materials could offer a revolutionary opportunity to develop advanced green batteries with large capacity, high energy and power d., and long cycle life.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpIYGAR_Y_SprVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lih8hoV8DmlaQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1MXktF2gt70%253D&amp;md5=ed7edff1f5d424cdd554631ae71ce2c1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref16" class="rightTabRefNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit16" data-doi="10.1039/c1ee01592c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Nano-Sn/C composites are ideal anode materials for high energy and power d. Li-ion batteries.  However, because of the low m.p. of Sn and the tendency of grain growth, esp. during high temp. carbonization, it was a significant challenge to create well-dispersed ultra-small Sn nanoparticles within a C matrix.  The authors demonstrate an aerosol spray pyrolysis technique, as a facile and scalable method, to synthesize a nano-Sn/C composite with uniformly dispersed 10 nm nano-Sn within a spherical C matrix.  The discharge capacity of nano-Sn/C composite sphere anodes maintains the initial capacity of 710 mA-h/g after 130 cycles at 0.25 C.  The nano-Sn/C composite sphere anodes can provide â¼600 mA-h/g even at a high rate of 20 C.  Such high rate performance for Sn anodes was not reported previously.  The exceptional performance of the nano-Sn/C composite is attributed to the unique nano-Sn/C structure: (a) C matrix offers mech. support to accommodate the stress assocd. with the large vol. change of nano-Sn, thus alleviating pulverization; (b) the C matrix prevents Sn nanoparticle agglomeration upon prolonged cycling; and (c) C network provides continuous path for Li ions and electrons inside the nano-Sn/C composite spheres.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrLYyFaDGc2HbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lih8hoV8DmlaQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhslGgtw%253D%253D&amp;md5=eb1dcc0d9d76d861eb8b2a90655d874b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref18" class="rightTabRefNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit18" data-doi="10.1021/am4023994"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lin, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Abel, P.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Here we provide the first report on several compns. of ternary Sn-Ge-Sb thin film alloys for application as sodium ion battery (aka NIB, NaB or SIB) anodes, employing Sn50Ge50, Sb50Ge50, and pure Sn, Ge, Sb as baselines. Sn50Ge25Sb25 also shows excellent rate capability, displaying a stable capacity of 381 mAhg-1 at a c.d. of 8500 mAg-1 (â¼10C).  A survey of published literature indicates that 833 mAhg-1 is among the highest reversible capacities reported for a Sn-based NIB anode, while 381 mAhg-1 represents the optimum fast charge value.  HRTEM shows that Sn50Ge25Sb25 is a composite of 10-15 nm Sn and Sn-alloyed Ge nanocrystallites that are densely dispersed within an amorphous matrix.  Comparing the microstructures of alloys where the capacity significantly exceeds the rule of mixts. prediction to those where it does not leads us to hypothesize that this new phenomenon originates from the Ge(Sn) that is able to sodiate beyond the 1:1 Na:Ge ratio reported for the pure element.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries offer an attractive option for low cost grid scale storage due to the abundance of Na.  Sn is touted as a high capacity anode for Na-ion batteries with a high theor. capacity of 847 mA-h/g, but it has several limitations such as large vol. expansion with cycling, slow kinetics, and unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation.  An anode consisting of a Sn thin film deposited on a hierarchical wood fiber substrate simultaneously addresses all the challenges assocd. with Sn anodes.  The soft nature of wood fibers effectively releases the mech. stresses assocd. with the sodiation process, and the mesoporous structure functions as an electrolyte reservoir that allows for ion transport through the outer and inner surface of the fiber.  These properties are confirmed exptl. and computationally.  A stable cycling performance of 400 cycles with an initial capacity of 339 mA-h/g is demonstrated; a significant improvement over other reported Sn nanostructures.  The soft and mesoporous wood fiber substrate can be used as a new platform for low cost Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrPjnqhdJ5q7rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljLT3pb4ZgitQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXosVegtr0%253D&amp;md5=b49c25ccb079701ddb3c8a22d15156ad</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref23" class="rightTabRefNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit23" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200346"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Performance of Porous Carbon/Tin Composite Anodes for Sodium-Ion and Lithium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. performance of mesoporous carbon (C)/tin (Sn) anodes in Na-ion and Li-ion batteries is systematically investigated.  The mesoporous C/Sn anodes in a Na-ion battery shows similar cycling stability but lower capacity and poorer rate capability than that in a Li-ion battery.  The desodiation potentials of Sn anodes are approx. 0.21 V lower than delithiation potentials.  The low capacity and poor rate capability of C/Sn anode in Na-ion batteries is mainly due to the large Na-ion size, resulting in slow Na-ion diffusion and large vol. change of porous C/Sn composite anode during alloy/dealloy reactions.     </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of improved rechargeable batteries represents a major technological challenge for this new century, as batteries constitute the limiting components in the shift from petrol (gasoline) powered to electric vehicles, while also enabling the use of more renewable energy on the grid.  To minimize the ecological implications associated with their wider use, we must integrate sustainability of battery materials into our research endeavours, choosing chemistries that have a minimum footprint in nature and that are more readily recycled or integrated into a full circular economy.  Sustainability and cost concerns require that we greatly increase the battery lifetime and consider second lives for batteries.  As part of this, we must monitor the state of health of batteries continuously during operation to minimize their degradation.  It is thus important to push the frontiers of operando techniques to monitor increasingly complex processes.  In this Review, we will describe key advances in both more sustainable chemistries and operando techniques, along with some of the remaining challenges and possible solutions, as we personally perceive them.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcS1itIL3E5u10loaUqgkFHkfW6udTcc2ebGyypmILpK6Lntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1c%252FmsVeiuw%253D%253D&amp;md5=55dc37b2186957b9c3f3f85f61d19fb5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref25" class="rightTabRefNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit25" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.6b03183"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pecher, O.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Carretero-GonzÃ¡lez, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Griffith, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review is presented.  Improving electrochem. energy storage is one of the major issues of our time.  The search for new battery materials together with the drive to improve performance and lower cost of existing and new batteries is not without its challenges.  Success in these matters is undoubtedly based on first understanding the underlying chemistries of the materials and the relations between the components involved.  A combined application of exptl. and theor. techniques has proven to be a powerful strategy to gain insights into many of the questions that arise from the 'how do batteries work and why do they fail' challenge.  In this special issue we highlight the application of solid-state NMR (NMR) spectroscopy in battery research - a technique that can be extremely powerful in characterizing local structures in battery materials, even in highly disordered systems.  An introduction on electrochem. energy storage illustrates the research aims and prospective approaches to reach these.  We particularly address 'NMR in battery research' by giving a brief introduction to electrochem. techniques and applications as well as background information on both in and ex situ solid-state NMR spectroscopy.  We will try to answer the question 'Is NMR suitable and how can it help me to solve my problem' by shortly reviewing some of our recent research on electrodes, microstructure formation, electrolytes and interfaces, in which the application of NMR was helpful.  Finally, we share hands-on experience directly from the lab bench to answer the fundamental question 'Where and how should I start' to help guide a researcher's way through the manifold approaches possible.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqSktNdpE_2crVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgfApsn8CFtyQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhslCkt7jM&amp;md5=d150b75264cb232248a15ba33c102dd5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref26" class="rightTabRefNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit26" data-doi="10.1038/nmat4577"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shpigel, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Levi, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A primary at.-scale effect accompanying Li-ion insertion into rechargeable battery electrodes is a significant intercalation-induced change of the unit cell vol. of the cryst. material.  This generates a variety of secondary multi-scale dimensional changes and causes a deterioration in the energy storage performance stability.  Although traditional in situ height-sensing techniques (at. force microscopy or electrochem. dilatometry) are able to sense electrode thickness changes at a nanometer scale, they are much less informative concerning intercalation-induced changes of the porous electrode structure at a mesoscopic scale.  Based on a electrochem. quartz-crystal microbalance with dissipation monitoring on multiple overtone orders, herein the authors introduce an in situ hydrodynamic spectroscopic method for porous electrode structure characterization.  This new method will enable future developments and applications in the fields of battery and supercapacitor research, esp. for diagnostics of viscoelastic properties of binders for composite electrodes and probing the micromech. stability of their internal electrode porous structure and interfaces.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpyvJhCwizB8bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgfApsn8CFtyQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XktVamsL0%253D&amp;md5=2d0cf6ab99a62b4cacd89ef8f3ecf059</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref27" class="rightTabRefNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit27" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.5b09553"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">ArÃ¡n-Ais, R.         </div><div class="casAbstract">To meet future needs for industries from personal devices to automobiles, state-of-the-art rechargeable lithium-ion batteries will require both improved durability and lowered costs.  To enhance battery performance and lifetime, understanding electrode degrdn. mechanisms is of crit. importance.  Various advanced in situ and operando characterization tools developed during the past few years have proven indispensable for optimizing battery materials, understanding cell degrdn. mechanisms, and ultimately improving the overall battery performance.  Here we review recent progress in the development and application of advanced characterization techniques such as in situ transmission electron microscopy for high-performance lithium-ion batteries.  Using three representative electrode systems-layered metal oxides, Li-rich layered oxides and Si-based or Sn-based alloys-we discuss how these tools help researchers understand the battery process and design better battery systems.  We also summarize the application of the characterization techniques to lithium-sulfur and lithium-air batteries and highlight the importance of those techniques in the development of next-generation batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqHnlNJxIyygLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgfApsn8CFtyQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXitVehtbo%253D&amp;md5=df2029292e0c37dacb6f5e1e9848197e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref29" class="rightTabRefNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit29" data-doi="10.1088/0034-4885/77/11/116502"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xie, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">SchlÃ¼cker, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Rationally Designed Multifunctional Plasmonic Nanostructures for Surface-enhanced Raman Spectroscopy: A Review</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Rep.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-sulfur (Li-S) batteries are highly appealing for large-scale energy storage.  However, performance deterioration issues remain, which are highly related to interfacial properties.  Herein, we present a direct visualization of the interfacial structure and dynamics of the Li-S discharge/charge processes at the nanoscale.  In situ at. force microscopy and ex situ spectroscopic methods directly distinguish the morphol. and growth processes of insol. products Li2S2 and Li2S.  The monitored interfacial dynamics show that Li2S2 nanoparticle nuclei begin to grow at 2 V followed by a fast deposition of lamellar Li2S at 1.83 V on discharge.  Upon charging, only Li2S depletes from the interface, leaving some Li2S2 undissolved, which accumulates during cycling.  The galvanostatic pptn. of Li2S2 and/or Li2S is correlated to current rates and affects the specific capacity.  These findings reveal a straightforward structure-reactivity correlation and performance fading mechanism in Li-S batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoja949rk5OxLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhx4sNDTpDWgw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhvVynsr3O&amp;md5=5d4fe0b1bf6fd1d31c329490da094bef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref43" class="rightTabRefNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit43" data-doi="10.1021/am500363t"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tokranov, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sheldon, B.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Precise in situ at. force microscopy (AFM) is used to monitor the formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on Si electrodes.  The stability of these passivation films on neg. electrodes is critically important in rechargeable Li-ion batteries, and high capacity materials such as Si present substantial challenges because of the large vol. changes that occur with Li insertion and removal.  The results reported here show that the initial rapid SEI formation can be stabilized before significant Li insertion into the Si begins and that the rate at which this occurs varies significantly with the nature of the surface.  The initial cycling conditions also have a substantial impact on the SEI that forms, with faster rates leading to a smoother, thinner SEI film.  To quant. interpret the SEI measurements, irreversible expansion of the Si during the first cycle was also monitored in situ with specifically designed specimen configurations.  On the basis of the exptl. results, relatively simple models were also used to describe the initial formation and stabilization of the SEI and to describe the relationship between the SEI thickness and expected SEI degrdn. mechanisms.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrmiAW86wovhbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhx4sNDTpDWgw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXkvVyrtbk%253D&amp;md5=a9a7fd0b1f20cd0aac7b3d9416b67517</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref44" class="rightTabRefNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit44" data-doi="10.1021/am505847s"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, X.-R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Deng, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yan, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guo, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wan, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Single Nanowire Electrode Electrochemistry of Silicon Anode by In Situ Atomic Force Microscopy: Solid Electrolyte Interphase Growth and Mechanical Properties</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) that forms on electrodes largely defines the performances of Li ion batteries (LIBs), such as cycling performance, shelf life, and safety.  Additives in the electrolyte can modify the properties of the SEI and thus efficiently improve the performances of LIBs.  However, the effects of additives on the mech. properties, structure, and stability of the SEI have rarely been studied directly.  The authors report the influence of vinylene carbonate (VC) and lithium bis(oxalate)borate (LiBOB) additives on the mech. properties of SEI films formed on MnO electrodes using at. force microscopy (AFM) and force spectroscopy.  The SEI formed from VC additive is thick and soft and partially decomps. upon charging.  LiBOB forms thin, stiff, and electrochem. stable SEI films, but the stiff SEI may not be favorable for adapting the vol. change of the electrodes.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Significant capacity losses are generally seen for batteries contg. high-capacity lithium alloy forming anode materials such as silicon, tin and aluminum.  These losses are generally ascribed to a combination of vol. expansion effects and irreversible electrolyte redn. reactions.  Here, it is shown, based on e.g. elemental analyses of cycled electrodes, that the capacity losses for tin nanorod and silicon composite electrodes in fact involve diffusion controlled trapping of lithium in the electrodes.  While an analogous effect is also demonstrated for copper, nickel and titanium current collectors, boron-doped diamond is shown to function as an effective lithium diffusion barrier.  The present findings indicate that the durability of lithium based batteries can be improved significantly via proper electrode design or regeneration of the used electrodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrsfUO_jKIgwbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhslIc8dXO4CQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXns1Sqsrk%253D&amp;md5=a65298e3393e78963e787aef9f44f36e</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i4">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.7b08870" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.7b08870</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Detailed experimental procedures and additional materials characterization (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b08870/suppl_file/am7b08870_si_001.pdf">am7b08870_si_001.pdf (2.01 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">
